## Episode 146: Do Expected Stock Returns Wear a CAPE?

### Key Points

- Kicking off with the fallout from the collapse of Archegos Capital, the death of Bernie Madoff, and the story of the $100 million New Jersey deli.
- Reflecting on the recent article, ‘Could Index Funds be ‘Worse Than Marxism’?’.
- On to today’s topic: do expected stock returns wear a cape?
- Theories for resolving the equity premium puzzle; either the model is wrong or the historical premium was higher than it will be in the future.
- Hear John H. Cochrane’s theory from his 1997 paper, ‘Where is the Market Going?’
- Why we can’t use historic US stock market data to approximate future premiums.
- Other issues with looking to past returns, like no proof that the equity premium was stationary.
- Why time periods characterized by decreasing risk should effectively see decreased discount rates too.
- Dimson, Marsh, and Staunton (DMS) on expected stock returns using out of sample data.
- Hear some of the equity risk premium stats from their world index versus the US.
- How annual returns have been relatively unaffected by global financial crises.
- From looking back, to what to expect going forward: the issues with interpreting past returns.
- Why, according to DMS, expected returns equal the growth rate in dividends plus the dividend yield.
- Hear the actual figures, which reflect the minor contribution of multiple expansion.
- What a company is worth if it doesn’t distribute capital to shareholders.
- Find out why the expected geometric equity risk premium works out to 3.5 percent.
- While the DMS approach is reasonable, it still doesn’t account for whether expected returns are constant through time or if they vary.
- Predictable stock returns dictate that changing risk aversion over time measurably affects risk premiums after good and bad events.
- Diving into the vast literature on return predictability, including a paper by Goyal and Welch.
- Why predictability is not as obvious as it seems, thanks to our sample data.
- What we can learn from ‘Long Horizon Predictability’ by Boudoukh, Israel, and Richardson.
- R-squared and market timing decisions; why it would need to be higher than it was historically.
- Hear about the world index analysis Benjamin performed and what it proves about risk premiums over 30 and 60 year periods.
- Bootstrap simulations and why they are criticized; because they ignore mean relationship, you get a much wider distribution of outcomes.
- Big breakthroughs from running through these numbers, like noting the upward bias and tighter distribution in long-run historical data.
- How to apply this on your own, using the 3.5 percent risk premium in the long run.
- Some of the other interesting things we noted during these simulations.
- We pull two cards: choosing between a holiday and a pet, and borrowing money with interest.
- Bad advice of the week: a free lunch-esque article on investing in private credit.

- “The investor’s worst enemy is himself”: Behavioral finance and investor psychology.
- “This time is different”: Templeton’s warning against market narratives and FOMO.
- “Have a philosophy you can stick with”: Why strategy persistence matters more than perfection.
- ARK as a case study: Conviction versus performance-chasing.
- Buffett on risk: Be ready for 50% drawdowns—even in diversified portfolios.
- The global market portfolio: Sharpe and Fama’s starting point for asset allocation.
- “Far more money is lost preparing for corrections”: Lynch on market timing mistakes.
- Volatility is emotional, not just mathematical—especially in crises like COVID or 2008.
- Charles Ellis: “Risk is not having the money when you need it.”
- “Volatility is the price of admission”: Embracing risk to pursue long-term returns.
- Ken Fisher: “Normal returns are extreme.” Why market behavior is rarely average.
- “Risk is what’s left when you think you’ve thought of everything.” Planning for the unknown.
- Life has a fat tail: LTCM and the perils of underestimating extreme events.
- “Make sure you’re at the table, not on the menu”: Cochrane on avoiding bad financial products.
- Bogle: “We get precisely what we don’t pay for.” Why low-cost beats high-fee.
- Trading and over-monitoring: Why “doing less” often means better returns.
- “It ain’t what you don’t know…”: Humility in the face of market uncertainty.
- “Diversification is the only free lunch”: Reducing risk without reducing expected return.
- Bogle: “Don’t look for the needle. Just buy the haystack.”
- Focus on what you can control: Savings, costs, asset allocation—not market returns.

## Episode 379: AMA #9 – Covered Call ETFs, Currency Hedging, and Bond Misconceptions

### Key Points

- Why dividend bias was relatively harmless, but the covered call craze is not—and how new ETFs “multiply like rabbits.”
- Ben’s analysis: in every example studied, covered call investors ended up with less wealth than those holding the underlying equities.

- Behavioral perspective: splitting the difference between hedged and unhedged can be the “strategy of least regret.”
- Bonds demystified—why falling prices during rising rates affect funds and individual bonds equally.
- Understanding duration: bond ETFs are designed to stay at a target maturity, while individual bonds age toward zero duration.
- How rising yields actually improve financial plans by boosting future expected returns.
- Choosing the right bond fund duration based on your time horizon and liabilities.
- Are recent bond losses an anomaly? Ben and Dan explain how decades of falling rates created unrealistic expectations.
- The role of unexpected rate changes in bond volatility—and why central banks don’t control long-term yields.
- Market-cap weighting: why it remains the most defensible way to allocate across countries and sectors.
- What’s changed their thinking after six years of Rational Reminder—from Scott Cederberg’s asset allocation data to the behavioral power of homeownership.
- The Horizons/Global X ETF debate: how swap-based, corporate-class structures create tax efficiency—and why that efficiency could vanish.
- Why PWL avoids these products: potential hidden tax liabilities and lack of transparency for clients.
- Borrowing to invest: Ben outlines why leverage works in theory—but Dan explains why most investors shouldn’t touch it.
- New “modest leverage” ETFs (125% exposure) as a more behavioral-friendly version of borrowing to invest.
- Fulfillment and frustration in finance: helping people achieve peace of mind vs. seeing deception still rampant in the industry.
- Five years of Vanguard’s all-in-one ETFs (like VEQT): how they’ve delivered exactly what they promised and reshaped DIY investing in Canada.
- Why these “one-ticket” portfolios remain the biggest innovation in Canadian investing—and why global diversification matters more than ever.
- Revisiting bonds in retirement: what to expect when they don’t offset stock volatility, and how to rethink risk management beyond yield-chasing.

## Episode 383: AMA #10 - Dollar cost averaging & mutual funds vs. ETFs

### Key Points

- Introduction – The first episode featuring all three hosts together: Ben Felix, Cameron Passmore, and Ben Wilson.
- OneDigital update: expanding evidence-based advice across Canada with new PWL partners in Halifax.
- The mission in motion – bringing the “markets work and planning matters” philosophy to more Canadians.
- “Finding and funding a good life” – how PWL integrates wellness and happiness into financial planning.
- AMA Question 1: Lump-sum vs. dollar-cost averaging — why lump-sum wins 65% of the time.
- Base rates, behavioral regret, and the real role of an advisor.
- The 2020 PWL paper results and how behavioral hedging fits in.
- If dollar-cost averaging feels safer, maybe your portfolio is too aggressive.
- AMA Question 2: Advice for smaller portfolios — how technology, AI, and fee-only planners can fill the gap.
- Can AI really replace advisors? Cameron’s Waymo analogy sparks debate.
- AMA Question 3: Mutual funds vs. ETFs — why in Canada, mutual funds may actually be more tax-efficient.
- The Capital Gains Refund Mechanism (CGRM) explained — and why it matters.
- Dimensional’s Canadian funds vs. Vanguard ETFs — tax distribution data that surprises most investors.
- AMA Question 4: Are discount bonds priced for tax efficiency? The evidence says no—discount bonds still win.
- AMA Question 5: Biggest investment mistakes — from Bitcoin regrets to house-buying reflections.
- AMA Question 6: Buffered ETFs — comfort, complexity, and why simple portfolios outperform.
- Simplicity as a superpower — why “markets work” is still the most radical idea in finance.
- AMA Question 7: Updating the RR model portfolio — why there’s no “optimal” portfolio and simplicity wins again.
- After show: Reviews, humor, and a reminder about “No Net Worth November.”
- Life offline — Cameron’s reflections on quitting social media and finding clarity.

## Episode 384: Mamdouh Medhat - A Profitability Retrospective, and Private Fund Performance

### Key Points

- Intro to Mamdouh Medhat and why his research fits the Rational Reminder “nerdy happy place.”
- The story behind Mamdouh’s retrospective paper with Robert Novy-Marx and the impact of the original profitability research on academia and practice.
- Three things the paper examines: quality investing, defensive/low-risk strategies, and value—unified through profitability.
- Why none of the 15 major academic and practitioner quality metrics add explanatory power beyond profitability.
- How spanning tests show profitability explains quality, but quality does not explain profitability.
- Quality measures largely load on profitability—they’re noisier versions of the same thing.
- The link between quality metrics and fundamental momentum, especially for QMJ and quarterly ROE.
- Practical implications: profitability is a parsimonious, more efficient way to capture the “quality” dimension.
- Defensive equity through the profitability lens—why high profitability predicts low volatility.
- Why long-only low-volatility strategies produce zero five-factor alpha—and why a simple high-profitability/low-investment portfolio plus T-bills beats them.
- Alternative value metrics (EBITDA/EV, intangible-adjusted book-to-market, etc.) don’t outperform price-to-book when profitability is accounted for.
- Many “improved” value metrics simply rotate in profitability exposure, not better value information.
- Roughly half of US value’s post-2007 underperformance is explained by its negative correlation with profitability.
- Industry tilts (e.g., energy/financials vs. tech/healthcare) drive much of value’s volatility—not its long-term return.
- The theoretical case for combining clean valuation (price-to-book) with clean expected cash flow (profitability).
- Academic implications: models must jointly explain value and profitability—and their negative correlation.
- Practitioner implications: parsimony—use clear valuation and cash-flow measures, limit excessive complexity.
- How Dimensional measures profitability: operating profitability (revenue – COGS – SG&A – interest) scaled by book equity.
- Why tilting toward or away from countries based on aggregate characteristics rarely adds value—premiums come from stocks, not countries.
- Industry-level tilts show similar patterns—industry momentum exists but is impractical due to massive turnover.
- How Dimensional handles country and industry weights: sort within countries, then apply sector caps.
- Private markets: private funds make up roughly 10% of the global investable universe—not 25–100% as sometimes claimed.
- Benchmark choice for private funds is crucial—S&P 500 is not appropriate for buyouts or VCs.
- Using KSPME (public-market equivalent), buyouts and VCs match small-cap value/growth benchmarks; private credit matches high yield; private real estate underperforms listed real estate.
- Factor exposures post-2007 explain 70–80% of private-fund return variation due to fair-value accounting.
- Wide dispersion in private-fund performance—top 5% double or triple capital; bottom 5% lose half.
- Little evidence of manager persistence—manager selection must rely on due diligence, not past vintages.
- No strong time trend in private-fund outperformance, but correlations with public markets have increased.
- Many diversification benefits historically attributed to private assets were actually illiquidity-driven smoothing.
- Rising demand and democratization likely reduce expected returns in private markets—exclusivity is fading.

## Episode 386: Is anyone doing dd? with Aravind Sithamparapillai

### Key Points

- Aravind’s introduction and reputation for deep, “pathological” research
- Why alts have become embedded in Toronto’s planning culture
- Client pressure, advisor FOMO, and the belief that 60/40 is “broken”
- Aravind’s personal path into indexing, factors, and Dimensional
- Why he started digging into alts: curiosity, client conversations, and advisor narratives
- The “conference meme”: why he asks questions others avoid
- The role of intellectual honesty vs. industry narratives
- The pivotal 2023 mortgage fund story: duration, turnover, and a major contradiction
- “Extend and pretend”: how stable NAVs can be manufactured
- What “gating” actually means and why it matters
- Marketing tactics: cherry-picked start dates and chart crimes
- IRR manipulation, vintage stacking, and anchoring bias
- Why comparing gross private credit returns to net equity returns is misleading
- The problem with “low correlation” as a selling point
- Why rebalancing with illiquid assets often fails in practice
- How Aravind builds expected return estimates for alts
- Private real estate: why expected returns often land near public market levels
- A case study: apparent outperformance disappears once you match the right benchmark
- The idiosyncratic risk of overweighting single-sector, single-region REITs
- Why most advisors don’t truly understand the all-in fees
- What real due diligence should include (and why it’s so hard)
- Should advisors trust third-party due diligence providers?
- How much comfort should investors take from audited financials?
- Why valuation levels (1–3) matter and why most private funds use Level 3 inputs
- The overall conclusion: markets work, but alts require extraordinary scrutiny

## Episode 387: Lessons from The Wealthy Barber (2025)

### Key Points

- Introduction — recording early and setting up a deep dive into the updated Wealthy Barber .
- Why the new 2025 edition lands so well: humor, modern references, and timeless lessons.
- Dave Chilton’s real-world insight from reviewing thousands of Canadians’ financial situations.
- Why the storytelling works — characters, humor, and accessible teaching.
- Inside the narrative: Roy the barber, Matt, Maddie, Jess, Kyle, and the barbershop regulars.
- Lesson 1: “You can do this” — personal finance isn’t about math, it’s about simple principles.
- Lesson 2: Save 10% and pay yourself first — habit beats theory, compounding does the rest.
- Why saving is hard today: algorithms, FOMO, lifestyle creep, and rising costs.
- The behavioral case for saving early, even if economists say otherwise.
- Lesson 3: Be an owner, not a loaner — stocks vs. bonds and the engine of human ingenuity.
- The investor’s paradox — the less you think you know, the better you invest.
- Why indexing wins: skewed stock returns and the impossibility of picking winners.
- How investing has changed since 1989 — indexing is now widely accessible.
- “The world feels scary today…” — the 1847 quote showing it always feels that way.
- RRSP vs. TFSA — identical outcomes at equal tax rates, and why RRSPs shine when taxed lower later.
- Debunking the RRSP “tax bomb” — why high earners still benefit most.
- Lesson 4: Housing — the four levers to buy today (cheaper homes, <20% down, 30-year amortization, FHSA/HBP).
- Why today’s young buyers need new strategies, not 1980s nostalgia.
- Longer amortizations: counterintuitive but often financially sound.
- Leverage vs. psychology — why borrowing to invest feels scary even when the math matches.
- Renting isn’t throwing money away — disciplined renters can match homeowner wealth.
- The hidden costs of owning — repairs, trees, chimneys, and constant surprises.
- The Canadian stigma around renting — and why it’s undeserved.
- Lesson 5: Spending — “faulty brain wiring,” social pressure, and unconscious habits.
- The multi-month spending summary — tedious but life-changing for both finances and happiness.
- Joy units per dollar — reallocating spending to maximize happiness.
- Practical rules: delay big purchases, beware car costs, indulge selectively, and remember “$1 saved = $2 earned.”

## Episode 388: AMA #11 - Your Parents' Advisor, 100% Equity Portfolios, and Investing $10 Billion

- Ben Wilson becomes a co-host — an unplanned evolution shaped by listener feedback.
- Dan on humility in forecasting and reconnecting with theoretical research.
- Ben’s personal year — firm acquisition, equity value jump, and navigating his cancer diagnosis.
- Talking to parents about high fees — emotional dynamics, non-confrontational questions, and the danger of implied judgment.

- Why PWL rarely optimizes asset location — complexity, low payoff, and behavioral clarity.

- Ben’s lessons as a new homeowner — maintenance costs far above expectations and the hidden burden of being your own contractor.
- The strangest portfolios — single-stock windfalls, leverage without client awareness, bullion-only strategies, and the infamous “meatloaf portfolio.”

## Episode 390: The "AI Bubble" and Stock Market Concentration

### Key Points

- What RR is about: evidence-based insights, synthesis episodes, expert interviews, and long-form inquiry — not debates.
- Why listeners value RR: transparency, friendly inquiry, returning to topics over time, and the hosts’ dynamic.
- Rising concern: clients asking whether U.S. market concentration and an AI bubble mean it’s time to exit stocks.
- Advisors echo similar worries: U.S. politics, all-time highs, and emotional decision-making.
- Today’s data point: Top seven U.S. stocks = 36% of S&P 500; 32% of the total U.S. market — highest on record.
- Why people fear concentration: a decline in the Magnificent Seven could meaningfully drag down the index.
- Canada’s cautionary tale: Nortel once hit 36% of the TSX — collapsed to zero — but the market recovered by 2005.
- Bubbles through history: canals, railways, fiber optics, dot-coms — innovation funded by speculation.
- Dot-com parallels: huge ideas, low cost of capital, lots of failures — but lasting infrastructure remained.
- AI dominance: Since ChatGPT, AI-linked companies drove 75% of S&P returns, 80% of earnings growth, 90% of capex.
- Reminder: No bubble calls — just context. High prices don’t equal an inevitable crash.
- Concentration vs. valuation: concentration shows weak links to future returns; valuations matter far more.
- Market timing trap: U.S. valuations were high in 2021 — selling then would have been disastrous.
- The U.S. lost decade: 2000–2010 returns were flat; in CAD, recovery didn’t happen until 2014.
- Value stocks held up: U.S. value and small-cap value delivered positive returns while broad indexes stagnated.
- Recency bias reminder: Canadians once avoided U.S. stocks entirely after a decade of underperformance.
- Japan 1989: World’s largest market crashes — still not recovered in real terms 36 years later.
- Global diversification wins: A 40% Japan-weighted global portfolio still performed fine thanks to U.S. growth.
- Cross-country data: Many markets are far more concentrated than the U.S. — still delivered solid returns.
- Valuation evidence: Higher CAPE = lower future returns — economically strong pattern across countries.
- Core lesson: Diversification + discipline. You will always hold winners and losers — that’s the point.
- Practical ways to lower concentration risk: global equity funds, small caps, and Canada’s 10% cap rule.
- Why active managers don’t help: only ~30–47% outperform depending on concentration trend.
- Final takeaway: high valuations may imply lower returns, but prediction is impossible — stay diversified.
- After-show review: Addressing a one-star critique (“Fartcoin Designer”) with humour and community context.

---

## Episode 391: How Assumptions Affect Financial Planning Outcomes

### Key Points

- Why this episode: recreating a conference panel on planning assumptions.
- Braden on the panel’s value for planners and DIY investors.
- Meet the guests: Adam, Joe, Aaron, and Braden.
- Assumptions matter: directional accuracy > prediction.
- Actuarial view: start with inflation, bond yields, and risk capacity.
- Engineering mindset: plan for expected and unexpected outcomes.
- Client pushback: longevity surprises and hidden assumptions.
- Asset allocation: strategic, goal-based, informed by behaviour.
- Software limits: life is too variable for perfect modeling.
- Behaviour gap: retirees spend less over time despite inflation.
- Software guides; planners interpret and humanize outputs.
- Use assumptions based on the specific question (e.g., withdrawals).
- Always ask: “Why are we modeling this?”
- Handling bias: reframe assumptions to reveal inconsistencies.
- Assumptions evolve: returns, spending, and research all change.
- Longevity beliefs: explore “why,” not just the data.
- Core truth: every plan is wrong — planning is iterative.
- When to update: depends on age, goals, and material changes.
- PWL approach: twice-yearly updates + adjustments during extremes.
- Tips: focus on behaviour, communication, goals, and integration.
- Success: relationships, impact, freedom, and sharing knowledge.

---

## Episode 392: The Rise of ETF Slop

### Key Points

- Introduction to the Rational Reminder Podcast and the hosts.
- Ben introduces the idea of “ETF slop” and why ETFs are no longer synonymous with sensible investing.
- More actively managed ETFs now exist than index-tracking ETFs in the U.S.
- ETFs increasingly engineered to attract assets rather than improve investor outcomes.
- Record ETF launches in 2025: over 1,000 in the U.S. and 300+ in Canada.
- Average management fees on newly launched ETFs rival traditional active mutual funds.
- The ETF “halo effect” and why structure is mistaken for quality.
- What an ETF actually is—and why it’s just a wrapper for a strategy.
- The first ETF was launched in Canada and still exists today.
- ETFs as tools for speculation versus long-term investing.
- Evidence that simpler allocation funds reduce harmful investor behavior.
- Why too much product choice can make good investing harder.
- Four categories of ETF slop introduced: thematic, buffer, covered call, and single-stock ETFs.
- Why thematic ETFs appeal to optimism and extrapolation bias.
- Evidence that most thematic ETFs underperform after launch.
- Morningstar data: almost no thematic ETFs outperform over long horizons.
- Why exciting narratives don’t translate into superior returns.
- Buffer ETFs explained: capped upside with partial downside protection.
- Research showing high fees, high costs, and inconsistent protection.
- Why simple stock/bond mixes dominate buffer ETFs even in drawdowns.
- Covered calls: high income today, lower total returns tomorrow.
- Why covered call ETFs systematically underperform their underlying assets.
- Income needs can be met more efficiently without covered calls.
- The cult-like following driven by double-digit yield marketing.
- Single-stock ETFs as the “sloppiest” form of ETF slop.
- Leveraged and inverse ETFs magnify volatility and complexity.
- Research showing massive underperformance versus simple benchmarks.
- Why these products resemble speculation more than investing.
- Complexity in investment products is strongly linked to poor outcomes.
- John Bogle’s warning: beware of new and “hot” investment products.
- Why ETFs are powerful tools—but only when used correctly.

## Episode 394: Equal Weight vs. Market Cap Weight Index Funds

### Key Points

- Introduction to Episode 394 and discussion about declining enthusiasm over long podcast runs.
- PWL Capital’s growing work with institutional clients and why index-based approaches are rare in that space.
- Episode topic introduced: equal-weighted index funds and why listeners keep asking about them.
- Definition of market-cap-weighted vs. equal-weighted indexes using the S&P 500 as the main example.
- Historical outperformance of equal-weighted S&P 500 indexes and why start dates matter.
- Equal weight vs. cap weight performance over the last decade: meaningful recent underperformance.
- Market concentration concerns and why equal weighting appears attractive during periods of high valuations.
- Why market-cap-weighted indexes do not mechanically buy more overvalued stocks as prices rise.
- Trading costs explained: explicit vs. implicit costs and why turnover matters more than TER.
- Capital gains, tax efficiency, and reporting differences between Canadian and U.S. funds.
- Market concentration historically shows little relationship with future returns.
- Volatility comparison: equal-weighted indexes are meaningfully more volatile due to small-cap exposure.
- Equal weighting increases exposure to small-cap, value, and high-volatility stocks.
- Sector distortions created by equal weighting and why this represents uncompensated risk.
- Unintended consequences: sector bets, security-level overweights, and forced rebalancing.
- Turnover is roughly 10× higher in equal-weighted funds than cap-weighted equivalents.
- Equal weighting behaves as a systematic anti-momentum strategy.
- Multi-factor regression results: positive size and value exposure, negative momentum loading.
- Rebalancing frequency trade-offs and how quarterly rebalancing amplifies momentum drag.
- Comparison with alternative approaches that target similar factor exposures more efficiently.
- Why backtests are seductive—and why live fund results matter more.
- Investor behavior, uncertainty, and the constant search for strategies that “fix” the market.
- Factor investing in disguise: most deviations from cap-weighting are just factor tilts.
- Equal weighting as an acceptable strategy—if investors understand and accept the trade-offs.
- Listener feedback, enthusiasm jokes, and discussion about Spotify video uploads and audio speed.

## Episode 395: Charles Chaffin - The Psychology of Financial Planning

### Key Points

- Introduction to Episode 395 and guest Dr. Charles Chaffin
- Charles’ background in financial planning psychology and authorship
- Why PWL wanted to move beyond the Grable–Lytton Risk Tolerance Scale
- Introduction to the Money and Risk Inventory (MRI) and full disclosure
- Announcement: Public access to a psychometric risk tolerance questionnaire
- Risk tolerance vs. risk capacity—and how PWL combines both
- Why firms must map risk scores to asset allocations themselves
- The role of psychology in financial planning beyond technical advice
- The Klontz–Chaffin model of financial psychology
- Why humans are “bad with money”: survival brains and emotions
- How heuristics and biases derail long-term planning
- Tools for overcoming bias: automation, pre-commitment, and friction
- How environment and social context shape financial behavior
- Financial flashpoints and their lasting impact on risk tolerance
- Financial self-efficacy and why low confidence leads to avoidance
- Money scripts: avoidant, worship, status, and vigilant
- Why understanding your own money scripts matters
- Common behaviors that lead to poor financial outcomes
- Practical strategies for recognizing and mitigating bad behaviors
- The role of identity in goal setting
- Why goals matter for motivation and behavior alignment
- Intrinsic vs. extrinsic goals and self-determination theory
- When quitting a goal is the right decision
- What financial risk tolerance really is
- Why people consistently misjudge their own risk tolerance
- How stable risk tolerance is over time—and what changes it
- Why reassessing risk tolerance regularly improves outcomes
- Handling couples with mismatched risk profiles
- Psychometric vs. revealed-preference risk questionnaires
- Evidence showing psychometric tools better explain real risk-taking
- Where traditional risk tolerance questionnaires fall short
